INK STAINS-Subscriber, City. It is
said that oxymuriatic acid removes per
fectly stains of Ink from paper. Nearly
all acids will remove spots of ink from
paper, but it is important to use oniy
such as do not attack its texture. Spirits
of salt (muriatic acid), diluted in five or
six times the quantity of water, may be
appli ed with success upon the spot a
minute or two after washing it with clean
water. "A solution of oxalic acid, citric
acid and tartaric acid is attended with
the least risk," says one who declares
he has used it, "and may be applied to
paper and printed plates -without fear of
damage. These acids, taking out writing
ink and not touching the printing, can
be used for restoring books where the
margins have been written upon with
out attacking the text." There are. sev
eral preparations for the purpose to be
obtained from -.stationers.

Fay J. Barnes began suit in the Jus
tices' Court, through her attorney, J. N.
E. Wilson, to recover ?250 damages which
Bhe claims to have sustained at the hands ;
of her brother-in-law. Charles H. Rogers,
on FebruaTy 6 of this year. In her com
plaint she alleges that Rogers struck
her and knocked her down and Infll< ted
Injuries that caused her much bodily and
mental agony and necessitated th<
vices of a physician for several weeks.
The day following the occurrence of the
events which form the basis of the com
plaint Rogers was arrested at the in
stance of Miss Barnes on a charge of bat
tery. The case was heard in the Police
Court and Rogers was fined $50.

The application of the Government for

States vs. David W. Jones was denied
yesterday by United States District Judge
de Haven. Jones had been sued by the
Government for damages for cutting tim
ber on public land in S'skiyou County and
had received a verdict in his favor, on
che erounds of error in the survey and
on the plea of the defendant that he
â believed that the timber was on his own

CIVIL SERVICE NEWS.
Internal Revenue Examinations Will
Be Conducted Hereafter Under
Auspices of Commission.
Internal Revenue Collector Lynch was
notified yesterday by the Civil Service
Commission that hereafter examinations
for positions in the Internal Revenue De
partment will be held by the department
at Washington, to which all applications
must be sent The examinations will be
held in this city as usual, with the dif
ference that the. local board, composed of
Special Revenue Agent Thomas, I
Fletcher and T. J. "Wrampelmeier. will be
abolished. These gentlemen ha
on the local board without pay for the
past four years and have been honored
by the thanks of the department.

THE SAN FKANCISCO CALL, WEDNESDAY, APRIL 26, 1899.
"SOAPY SMITH" WILL
BE THE LADIES' PET
A FREAK FOR THE DOG SHOW.
SOAPY SMITH, a dog owned by A. Carrillo of this city, will without doubt
be the greatest curiosity of any dog that will be exhibited in the com
ing dog show. Soapy is a recent arrival from the Klondike region; in
fact, he only arrived from the frozen north two weeks ago, and was sent
down by express to Carrillo by a friend as a present. The owner says that
he dors i:i. t want any more canine presents, as he had to pay $125 express on
the animal. The most curious thing about the dog is that he is half bear and
half dog. His father was a big brown bear and his mother a genuine Klon
dike dog. Soapy has inherited his father's nn<> fur coat, the nair on his back
being several inches long. He also walks with the clumsy gait of a young
cub. "nut he is quite docile and tame. How he will stand the warm weather
1. but the hot spell of last week was very hard on the
animal. If the temperature should be warm for Soapy out at the show his
owner claims he will treat him to cold storage treatment by placing a big
cake of Ice in his kennel. Several Klondike miners who have seen the dog
claim that the animal is a splendid specimen of his species and that this breed
of animals, though only recently attempted in the north, has proved a great
success and that ior hard work drawing sleighs they have more endurance
and patience than any other breed.

McKELVEY WAS
NOT ON THE
MARY GARRET
Mourned as Among
the Dead.
A DISAPPEARANCE EXPLAINED
WENT TO THE KOOTENAI AND
NOT THE KLONDIKE.
A Wealthy Oakland Mining Oper
ator Given Up as Lost Returns
to His Friends and Busi
ness Associates.
After beinp mourned as dead for nearly
it John McKelvey, the wealthy Oak
land mining man, has come back among
his friends and business associates on
this and the other side of the bay, and is
l" Ing kept busy explaining his mysteri
ous disappearance of eleven months ago.
Through all these months McKelvey has
been remembered as one of those who
went down on the ill-fated Mary Carret,
the Klondike boat that turned turtle and
sunk With nearly an on board last May
when on her way to St. Michael. His sud
den reappearance has brought joy to a
host of friends he has made since his res-
Idence in California in this city and Oak
land.
McKelvey came to this country from
Mexico some live or six years a^o, and
since that time has been heavily inter
ested in mining properties on both the
northern and southern sections of the
mother lode, and a number of them he
has had under successful operation, lie
had the inborn shrewdness of the canny
Scott, and his success in his mining ven
tures made him many influential busi
n< ss associates.
When the Klondike fever was at its
height he bought interests in four of the
best claims in that district and started a
couple of experienced miners on their way
there to manage his new properties. They
took passage on the schooner Mary
Garret
He made, his plans known to few of his
friends, but those who were informed
understood that he was to take passage
on the same vessel. At the time she
Bailed he also disappeared, and when the
Dews earn.' back from the Alaskan coast
that, with others of her class, the Mary
Garrett had sunk with nearly all on
board McKelvey was given up as one
among the lost. Then came the list of
drowned, and the name "John McKelvey"
appeared among them, and it was an
nounced that the only ones saved were
a minister and his wife. The minister has
since been confined in a madhouse as. a
result of his awful experience.
McKelvey's name 'appearing among the
list of lost settled his fate in the minds
of his friends, and they were about ready
to set about arranging his estate when
he came back to arrange It himself. He
got back on Wednesday last, and his
surprise was great at learning his obit
uary had been already written.
He had simply started off on one of his
periodical prospecting trips, and it had
taken him into the Kootenal district of
British Columbia. He had not thought It
worth while to announce his trip, and
following his usual custom he had started
off after saying nothing.
It has developed since that there was a
John McKelvey on the passenger list of
the lost Mary Garrett and that he lost
his life with others who went down with
her, but he was not the McKelvey who
was mourned for so long a time, and all
the search possible has failed to find any
one to mourn for him. No trace has betn
found of his relatives or acquaintances,
and so far as the history of that ill
advised rush is concerned he will be
known as simply one of the many who
gave up their lives to it.

Moving the Fence.
The sign fence, a nuisance which sur
rounds the ruins of the Baldwin Hotel,
and which has for months past been an
obstruction to pedestrians, is to be re
moved. By a special permit from the
Board of Supervisors "Lucky" Baldwin
was allowed to erect the fence, and when
he did so extended it so far out from the
walls on the . Market and Ellis street
sides as to take up more than half of the
pavement. In the case of the Market
street side this has been a great annoy
ance, as it left a very narrow passage
way.
At the meeting of the Street Commlt
tee on Monday night positive orders were
given to have the fence moved back to
the line of the building. Yesterday Bald
win, in compliance with the order, set a
gang of men to work to move the fence
back. The work will be accomplished
this week, much to the convenience of
all pedestrians and the joy of the shop
keepers in the neighborhood, to whom it
has been a great inconvenience.
BATTLE OF BUNKER HILL.
Its Anniversary Will Be Fittingly
Cele orated in This City.
The anniversary of the Battle of Bunker
Hill will be celebrated In an elaborate
fashion by patriotic societies of this city
on June 17. For the past forty years the
day has been fittingly recalled in San
Francisco by speech and song, but this ]
year it is proposed to make the details
of the celebration more extensive and to
excel all past commemorations of Its
character. The Sons of the American
Revolution jvill unite with the Califor- |
nia Pioneers and the Bunker Hill Asso- \
ciation, and they will invite the partici- i
pation of public men in exercises which
will be arranged to-night.
At Sunset Park, in the Santa Cruz
Mountains, an appropriate programme
will be listened to, and two trainsâ one
at 7:45 a. m. and the other at 8:50â will
leave the foot of Market street for the
grounds. Two bands will accompany the
excursionists.
This evening a meeting of the three so
cieties will be held at the Occidental Ho
tel to arrange a programme. It has been
proposed to request the Secretary of War
to fire a salute from all the forts in the
harbor, and Mayor Phelan will be invited
to t.V-p a prominent part in the pro
gramme. The Governor v.-ill be asked to
declare the 17th day of June a legal holi
day, whereby business may be suspended.
MINERAL LANDS.
Judge Morrow Renders an Important
Decision as to the Duty of Govern
ment to Prove Certain Facts.
United States Circuit Judge Morrow de
cided Monday that when the Govern
ment brings suit to cancel a land grant
under the plea that the land is more val
uable for its mineral deposits than for Its
agricultural products, the Government
must prove the plea.
The case was that of the T T nited States
against the Central Pacific Railroad Com
pany, George W. Towle, Allen Towle, Ed
win Towle and others to cancel a grant
by the United States to the Railroad Com
pany of a section of timber land in Ne
vada County. It appeared from the evi
dence that some mining had been done on
the land before thr- Government granted it
to the railroad company, but that the !
mining had been discontinued, and after
the Towles bought the section from the
railroad they cut a large quantity of val
uable timber off the tract.
The Government failed to prove that th*e
land was more valuable for its mineral
deposits than for its timber, etc., hence
it lost the case.

THE GREAT HISTORICAL PANORAMA,
BATTLE OF
MANILA BAY
Market street, near Eighth.
OPEN DAILY FROM 9 a. m. to 10 p. m.
ADMISSION. 50c; Children, 25c.
This panorama is a correct representation of
the great fight. The landscape, bay and ships
have been painted by artists purposely sent to
Manila; and returning naval officer* and others
have assisted by furnishing correct data, thas
enabling the management to reproduce the
battle as it was fought by Admiral Dewey,
historically and absolutely correct in every de-
tail. Same general style as Waterloo and
Gettysburg, but surpassing them in artistic
merit and interest, showing not only the bat-
tle but the beautiful blue Bay of Manila
with its villages and cities along the shores.

THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, WEDNESDAY, APRIL 20, 1899.
HE CUT HIS THROAT
UNDER A FLATCAR
HASKEL CANTROWITZ, the 20-year-old son of Heyman Cantrowitz of
514 Fuiton street, was found dead yesterday morning under a flatcar on
a sidetrack near the Southern Pacific railroad station at Colma, San
Mateo County, his throat cut from ear to ear. A bloody razor
clenched in a death grip in the right hand warranted the presumption that the
young man taken his own life. The body was found at 5 o'clock in the morn
a resident of Colma named Charles Sundima.n, who was walking along
the track, lie notified Deputy Coroner Cunningham at Baden. At an inquest
held later the jury returned a verdict of suicide. The remains were taken to
od City.
The identity of the suicide was established from a letter on his person, and
the members of his family in this city were notifl* d of tho fate that had over
taken him. They were at a loss to account for his act. one of them saying
that there was no known reason for it, as he had everything he desired. It is
ible, however, that the mind of the young man, who was a sufferer from
erysipelas and extremely sensitive, had become unbalanced from inability to
bis position. He was <riven a place Monday a week ago in the receiving
department of Weinstock, Lubin & Co., with the promise, if he proved capa
ble, of a permanent position. He had been employed by the firm in another
department during the busy holidays and at several other dry goods stores in
the city, and had given satisfaction. He did not appear, however, to be able
to fulfill the requirements of his new position and he was discharged last Sat
urday night. Monday morning he left his home and that is the last his family
saw or hoard of him until informed of his suicide.
U. S. GRANT IS
NOT DISTURBED
BY IDLE RUMOR
The San Diego States
man Is in Town.
Governor Gage has announced that he
Will appoint the three Paris Exposition
Commissioners as Boon as he returns to
Sacramento from the San Pedro Harbor
Jubilee. According to the story wired
to' this city the Commissioner from
Southern California will be a Grand
Army man. The inference is drawn there
fore that Major Ben C. Truman will be
appointed. lit- is a Grand Army com
rade who served under General George
H. Thomas with the Army of the Cum
berland. It is not called to mind that
there is any other aspirant of the order
In the field for this particular place,
hence the conclusion is strengthened that
-Major Ben will get the job.
At last accounts the major enjoyed the
friendship of Collls P. Huntingdon. When
other magnates of the Southern Pacific
consented to the scheme to oust the ma
jor from the literary department of the
company Huntingdon remained . loyal to
his old friend and carried his loyalty to
the extent of protesting against the sac
rifice of Truman. The late A. N. Towne
was another stanch admirer of the major
and never conceded that any one on this
side of the continent possessed a literary
style which surpassed that which the
major exhibited in the early numbers of
Del Monte Wave. When the Southern
Pacific threw down the major the Santa
Fe took him up and most likely the pro
gressive corporation has used Its Influ
ence in his behalf.
The story published a few days ago
that the Governor had appointed D. M.
Burns to be United States Senator from
California is not verified by the facts, as
no appointment has been made and none
will be made until the Senate renders a
decision in the Quay case. No doubt
the Governor will appoint Burns if he
finds that an appointee will be Heated.
The same railroad influence that was in
voked to procure the election of Burns
is doubtless employed to bring about his
appointment, but Gage will do nothing
but promise until the Senate acts on
Quays commission.
U. S. Grant arrived in the city from
San Diego yesterday morning and regis-
red at the Palace Hotel. During the
day he saw several of his leading sup
porters in the recent Senatorial campaign
at Sacramento. Grant is not at all dis
turbed by the rumor that Gage intends
to appoint Burns to fill tTie vacancy In
the Senate. He is not a candidate him
self for the honor of appointment by tne
Governor, and if he were then; is no cer
tainty that Gage would appoint him.
Nothing disturbs Mr. Grant. He is likely
to attain distinction in California as the
I great undisturbed. He will remain in
an Francisco for several days
Joseph L. Maude of Pasadena, the
â¢â¢ founder of the Bureau of Highways, is
,In the city en route to Sacramento. He
is an aspirant for the office of Highway
Commissioner and has the indorsement
of very many members of the Legisla
ture. Among his indorsers are men who
voted for Grant and others who voted
for Burns. Aside from these, he has the
indorsement of many engineers. he
Commissioner to be appointed by Gov
ernor Gage will have a voice in direct
ing the disbursement of $55,000. The
Legislature appropriate $25,000 for a
wagon road from Placerville to Lake
Tahoe and also $25,000 for Mono road.
There Is a Federal appropriation of $5000
to explore for the 'site of a new road into
Yosemite Valley. This sum is to be ex
pended by a man appointed by the Sec
retary of the Interior to represent the
Geological Survey, an army officer ap
pointed by the Secretary of War and by
the California Commissioner of High
ways. -

McKINLEY'S COAST VISIT.
Pressure of Official Business May-
Prevent the President's West
ern Tour.
President McKinley may not be able
after all to visit the Pacific Coast this
year. Some time ago it was announced
that the President was preparing to make
a tour of Inspection of the coast and to
visit most of the Important Western cit
ies. The authorities of several towns in
the State were outlining plans for a grand
reception, and it was accepted as a prac
tical certainty that the President would
come. It appears now, however, that the
President may not be able, owing to the
pressure of public business, to leave
Washington.
This information was received yester
day by a gentleman who had communi
cated with President McKinley to learn
his plans and his wishes. The letter was
answered by the assistant secretary- of
the President, and in part was as fol
lows:
"It is still quite uncertain whether the
pressure of public business will permit
the President to visit the Pacific Coast
this year, and as yet no plans whatever
have been made for such a trip."
This official announcement from the
household of the President himself settles
all further question In reference to the
matter. San Franciscans may not have
the pleasure of seeing the chief executive
this year.

CRIMMINS OUT
FOR DIRECTOR
HEAD'S SCALP
Would Deprive Him of All His
Chairmanships.
To-Night's Meeting Will Very Likely Change the
Old Balance of Power in the Board
of Education.
FROM the headquarters of Mr. Phillip
Crlrnmins the ukase has gone forth,
and unless publicity fihall change or
postpone for a time the plans of the
Board of Education, Director Edward
Head will to-night be shorn of the chair
manship of the Supplies Committee and
will be cut down from the same office on
the Evening Schools Committee.
Mr. Head, it is said, is to be relieved
for cause. The charges against him are
incorporated in a repor.t which is due to
be presented at the meeting of the board
this evening and which have been in
preparation for the past fortnight. The
Crimmins six on the board claim that
the decent element, frequently mentioned
as the "Club Five," will vote with them
for Mr. Head's dethronement.
The report will set forth that Head is
relieved of his executive prerogatives
because he has found not enough time
to properly administer the affairs of ftis
offices. The Supplies Committee is one
of the most important in the city's plan
of schools. To it come all the requisi
tions of the different principals for the
supplies necessary to their different
charges, and the chairman's days are
full in attending to them.
Mr. Head's duties as an employe of the
Spring Valley Water Company, so it is
paid, do not leave him sufficient time for
Supply Committee matters. The Evening
School Committee is of almost equal im
portance, and Mr. Head's Spring Valley
duties have apparently left him little
time for that, either, for the fact remains
that in the four months of his incum
bency he has rendered but one report]
and that upon evening school matters.
These art- the things, it is said, that
are to be charged against Mr. Head. At
any rate they will appear upon the re
port when that shall be rendered, and in
throwing him out of his position his fel
low members will find their excuse in
them.
It appears that Head's undoing is his
own, and it began when the present
board was organized last December. At
that time ho started to make the worst
of his splendid opportunities. The first
nlan of organization was promulgated by
Mr. Stow and it gave to the clean ele
ment on the board control of adminis
trative matters. Besides Mr. Stow as
president, Mr. Eels (D. ) was to be chair
man of the Classification Committee and
Mi Stow's best man In all board poll
tics. Stafford was to be chairman of the
Supplies Committee, Brandenstein of the
Committee on Rules and Holbrook was to
have the desk job on the Finance Com
mittee.
Mr. Stow's slate was made public on
an ill-chosen day and the Crimmins faction
had ample time to work up a scheme to
beat it. It chose Bcrgerot as its presi
dent and Armstrong. Kemp, Cole and
Gedge for the chairmanships. In neither
one of these schedules was Head consid
ered, and between the two he was left

I $1 per Year.NAVAL OFFICERS
WILL COMMAND
THE TRANSPORTS
Capt. Lombard Takes
the Morgan City.
FOR GRAIN-CARRYING SHIPS
A FRENCH DECISION FOLLOWS
THE AMERICAN LAW.
Captain Gage of the Al-Ki Now Has
the Walla Walla â George Dry
den Appointed Assistant
Chief Wharfinger.
Captain John E. Lombard went out in
command of the Morgan City last night, \
vice Captain Dillon, who has retired. Cap
tain Lombard is well known on the coast,
having made his home here for several
years. He Is a native of Maine and en
tered the merchant marine when quite a j
boy. On the outbreak of the Civil War (
he enlisted in the navy and was in the!
Jleet commanded by Admiral Farrag\it. He
was in several actions, including that of
New Orleans, and was wounded in the leg '
at Fort St. Philip. At the close of the war i
ho was retained In the navy, but resigned
some years ago. Tho captain saw service j
in the Spanish war, being at the battle of
Manila as executive officer of the U. S. S. :
Kaleigh.
Captain Lombard holds the rank of lieu
tenant In the navy.
The other officers of the Morgan City
are: First mate, Thomas P. Dorris second
mate, Kenealy, and third mate, Peter Ole
sen. The transport sailed for Manila late
yesterday afternoon with some tXXt recruits
and lifteten civilians.
Captain Stenhouse of the British ship
Talus when at Havre, France, with a car- !
go ot" wheat became involved In a law suit, |
which is of interest to all masters of ves
sels carrying rain to foreign ports. Ac
cording to the custom, the sacks contain
ing; the wheat had been cut so that the i
grain would run down and till all the in- |
terstices, making the cargo into a solid
mass and thus prevent it from shifting.
The merchants to whom the wheat had
been consigned brought suit against Cap
tain Stenhouse for damage to the wheat
and also to the sacks. The sacks, they
claimed; had been shipped in good condi
tion and on arrival had been found cut.
causing the loss of grain. Thereupon they ;
brought suit against Captain Stenhouse
tor one franc for each short sack and BuO
iranc= for sacks damaged, or 4100 francs '
In all. The Judge rendered a decision in
favor of the merchants and based his de
cision on a judgment given in a case sim
ilar to this one In the United States and
tried in ISO 3.
Captain J. T. Gage has been given com
mand of the steamer Walla Walla, owned
by the Puclllc Coast Steamship Company.
ll'iS appointment comes in the nature of a
surprise. It will be remembered that Cap
tain Gage commanded the steamer Al-Ivi,
which had a collision with the steamer
Homer in Seattle. The Homer was com
manded by Captain Jessen. The Seattle
inspectors'held that Jessen was liable and
Gage was not In fault. Then the case
was submitted to the San Francisco in
spectors, with the result that the former
decision was overruled, JesÃen was exon
erated and Gage was supposed to lose his
license for six months. Now J. T. Gage
has been given command of the steamer
Walla Walla.
The City of Papeete arrived yesterday
from Tahiti in the remarkably long pas-
Face of forty-three and a half days. She
generally makes the voyage In thirty
three to thirty-four days. The Papeete
brought up the largest load of copra that
has ever been received here, the amount
of copra arriving representing the shred
ding of from 40,000,000 to 50,'j00.000 cocoa
nuts. Everything is reported quiet in
Tahiti. The war scare has long since
passed away, and it is said that the Gov
ernor of the islands has received a severe
reprimand for his action in placing the
town of Tahiti in a posture of defense.
It will be rem.-mbered that the Governor
actually believed that war had broken out
between England and France and so pre
pared for a vigorous defense against the
attack of English war vessels. He brought
the French gunboat alongside the quay,
dismounted her guns, placed them on the
heights, issued rations for ninety days to
the Inhabitants and established interior
lines of defense against the enemy that
never came. His patriotism has resulted
In a severe official wigging.

POPULAR ENTHUSIASM AROUSED OVER
THE EXTENSION OF THE PANHANDLE
Thousands of Workmen, Laborers, Artisans and Me
chanics Will Find Employment in the Gigantic
Undertaking to Improve the City.
MAP OF THE PROPOSED EXTENSION OF THE PARK PANHANDLE.
FROM present indications it will
be impossible to hold a special
bond election before next De
cember to determine whether or
not the people of the city wish
an elaborate municipal system of sew
ers, adequate public buildings, hos
pitals and the extension of the pan
handle of Golden Gate Park to Van
Ness avenue. The preliminary work of
preparation for such an election is mi
i tricate and cannot be hastened, al
though the local authorities are work
ing as rapidly as possible. Plans, speci
fications, estimates and rough ap
praisements must be made before the
election can, under the law, be held.
Public sentiment Is rapidly crystalliz
ing into an eager advocacy of the pro
posed improvements, particularly that
of the extension of the panhandle.
This support is being given more es
pecially by the generality of the people,
by men and women of the middle
classes, by those in moderate circum
stances and the poor. To these classes,
who constitute the great bulk of the
population of San Francisco, the ex
tension of the panhandle means an
inestimable benefit as yet but poorly
understood even by those who value it
most.
The tremendous undertaking which
the Mayor and the Board of Supervis
ors are projecting is meeting also with
the favor of the residents' of the out-
Bide districts, who will be benefited by
the accession of new sewers, hospitals
and other incidental improvements.
The extension of the panhandle appeals
particularly to the people In the inside
districts. Those living south of Market
street, down town as well as in the
Mission, will be benefited quite as much
as those residing north of Market
street.
For people In every part of the city j
THE SAN FRAKCISCO CALL, WEDNESDAY, APEIL 26, 1899.
a new park domain will be created for
recreation, enjoyment, fresh air and
health. An erroneous opinion seems to
have gained some currency that the
extension of the panhandle will mean
simply that those who can afford to
drive to Golden Gate Park will have a
longer drive and that people who need
a park will be neglected. This Is alto
gether an error. Whatever objection
has been made to the extension of the
panhandle has come from wealthy men
who balk at a public Improvement
when that improvement touches their
pockets. These men look upon the pan
handle as It exists now from Stanyan

street to Baker street as a necessary
evil. Most of them do not utilize it on
their way to the park, but drive out
Fell street.
They are not concerned, therefore, in
having the panhandle extended and
they have not been Blow to utter a
protest because the proposed extension
will cost them a considerable sum of
money. They are not concerned in the
fact that the tremendous undertaking
of extending the panhandle will give
thousands of workmen, laborers, arti
sans and mechanics employment for
years. It will take time and money to
effect the great change and to trans
form a little city Into a great garden.
This money will be paid to the very
men for whose benefit the new park is
to be created and the coin will be paid
very larg* ly by the men who drive now
in their splendid teams to Golden Gate
Park.
In order that the character of the
change to be. made by the proposed ex
tension of the panhandle may be
thoroughly understood a map of the
projected improvement is published
this morning. The present panhandle
begins at Stanyan street' and ends at
Baker street. The extension from that
point to Van Ness avenue between
Oak and Fell streets is natural and in
a straight line. Long before the city
was what it is now such an extension
was planned and advocated. "When the
site of the City Hall was a cemetery
the creators of Golden Gate Park, with
almost prophetic vision, saw that some
day Golden Gate Park must start in
the very heart of the city and then take
its direct course to the ocean beach.
But these men had to fight stubborn
ly for years to get Golden Gate Park,
and at last they were forced to give
way and sacrifice the splendid park
that would have begun at Stanyan
street and ended at the City Hall. Only
the strip from Stanyan to Baker was
saved and it remains to-day to show
what the people of the city ought to do.
Under the proposed plan of extension
the new panhandle will bo in every
sense a great and beautiful park. It
will be ample to provide occupation for
the thousands that now seek Golden
Gate Park. It will be adorned with
statuary, fountains, arbors and a floral
wealth which has made Golden Gate
Park one of the great garden spots of
the world. In every sense it will be a
park stretching block after block
toward the west untjl it broadens out
into the splendid domain of the park
proper.
It is this prospect which has caught
and will hold popular enthusiasm until
the day when with ballots the voters
of the city decide to make the prospect
a reality and to acquire a new recrea
tion ground which will be without
parallel among the cities of America
and perhaps of the world.
TO ASSESS GOODS NOT
HERETOFORE TAXED
ADVISORY EXPERT PLATT TO
SUBMIT HIS REPORT.
Personal Property Assessment Com
mittee of the Supervisors Will
Meet To-Bay to Take Offi
cial Action.
The personal property assessment
committee of the Board of Supervisors
will hold a meeting to-day in order to
hear the report of Advisory Expert Al
fred G. Platt regarding certain property
which has heretofore escaped taxation.
Mr. Platt will submit a tabulated state
ment of such properties and the commit
tee will decide whether it will be advis
able to turn the data over to Assessor
Dodge or submit it to the Board of
Equalization, which meets on the first
Monday In July.
Mr. I'latt Js extremely desirous of act- !
ing in harmony with the Assessor, al
though the latter declined to appoint him
as a deputy In order to arrive at the con
clusions which he has embodied in his
report to the committee. The Assessor
has everything in hand at the present S
time with regard to assessments, and Air. i
]'!;Ut's duties consisted in learning how I
much property has missed being taxed.
Should the report be turned over to the
BOr he ran make the additional as- I
sessmonts accordingly, lie Is liable un
der under his bond if he does not make
a proper asw ssment, and the informa
tion of Mr. Platt will puido him in arriv
ing at correct valuations. Mr. l'latt's re
will be a comprehensive review of
the situation.
In regard to merchandise in bond, the
opinion which was recently handed down
by the City and County Attorney is rath
er equivocal, and it is difficult to iix the
taxation on such goods in the hands of
the original importer and in the original
packages. As soon as a package is brok- j
en, however, it enters Into the merchan- !
dlse of the State and as such must be
taxed.
In the case of goods bottled In bond
they would come under the head of pri
vate bonded warehouses and as such
would bo taxable. In the matter of whis
kies withdrawn from the internal reve
nue bonded warehouse In Kentucky, for
example, and exported for sale in foreign
markets and brought back to the United
a and placed in a bonded warehouse
at a port of entry, It is held that g
of that class are not in the strict sense
Imported from a foreign country and in
fact are not treated as such by the Gov
ernment, which exacts only the internal
revenue tax and not the duty imposed on
foreign liquors. This point has not as
yet been definitely settled by the Su
premo Court, but the contention is that
such reimported liquors should be taxed
as personal property.
The national banks come in for a share
of Mr. Platt's attention in his report. The
act regulating their operations provides
that such banks shall be obliged to invest
in Government bonds to the extent of 90
per cent of their circulating issue. This is
a technical loan to the Government, and if
the funds cannot be taxed the shares of
Btock earning dividends can be. Mr. Platt
is now tabulating the reports filed by the
national banks with tho Bank Commission
en March 4 with a view to assessing on
something more than the franchises, on
which they have only paid taxes in the
past.
With regard to the premiums outstand
ing and in process or collection by fire,
marine and lifo insurance companies in
corporated j n foreign countries. It is ex
pected that largo revenues will bo derived
therefrom, if they are held to be solvent
( redlts. It is a fact that $6,000,000 are sent
away from the Pacific Coast every year
which never pays a cent of tax. Consid
ering that the lire insurance companies
have suffered only a 28 per cent loss ratio
during the past twenty years, it seems but
fair that they should bo compelled to bear
the slight burden of V/ 2 per cent taxation
for State purposes. They reap the benefits
of fire, police, judiciary and other pro
tections, but have never contributed to
their maintenance', at least by paying tax
es on what are held to be solvent credits.
POOL-SELLING CASES.
The Batch in Judge Conlan's Court
Are Continued Till May 10 to
Be Set.
The cases of H. L. Jones and others, ar
rested at the racetrack for violating the
new poolselling ordinance, were called in
Judge Conlan's court yesterday to be set
for trial. Chief Lees and Captain Witt
man were in court.
Attorney Ach, for the defendants, moved
that the cases be continued for a year,
as there would be no more racing at the
Ingleslde track, but Prosecuting Attorney
Low retorted that such a motion was
ridiculous, and the Judge promptly re
fused It. After argument the Jones case
was continued till May 10 to be set.
The Judge, in speaking of the matter
later, said: "I was willing at the first call
ing of the cases to try onft, but the police
told me that as the arrests were made on
the second day the cases in my court were
not so strong as those in Judge Mogan's
court, and they wanted mine continued,
which has been done from time to time.
To my surprise, the police came into my
court this morning and said they were
ready to proceed and asked that the casea
be set. I am going on my annual vaca
tion at the end of the month and there
foro cannot try the rases, but I have set
them for May iv and the pollco can got an
other Judge to come into my court and
try them. Judge Low had never been con
sulted by an official of the Police Depart
ment frc-m the first calling of the cases."

Mexican Dollars higher.
Silver continues to advance.
New York exchange declined.
Wheat futures lower.

Hay and Feedstuff as before.
Beans quiet and generally easy.

California!] fruits are steady, says the Lon
don Grocers' Gazette, but with supplies mostly
cut of first hands, and with the wholesale
and retail trade getting deliveries of their
previous purchases, transactions are not num
erous. Business in salmon has been rather ills
appointing, end the principal portion of ar
rivals are belnt; stored for demand, which is
likely to arise with tiner weather. a fairly
pood trade has continued in half-pound tins,
and supplies are being distributed to retailers.
Prices are well maintained.

THE OUTLOOK FOR WHEAT.
The Toledo Market Report says: "It is
not a. "bullish" feature that spring wheat seed
ing r.i â¢> promises to be earlier than last year.
It is a strong point gained in favor of a crop
corresponding to last year, it is not a "bull
ish' feature that cur export movement has
largely decreased. That movement last week
was less than 30 per cent of the world's ship
ments. The seaboard reports daily sales, but
they are more for covering previous 'short'
sales by foreigners than for export. Crop
killing reports have advanced American prices
above a parity with England. It is not a
"bullish" feature that a 1 great number of mills
In the winter wheat area are closed, because
no rrarket for flour can be found, even at cost
on present wheat prices. It is not a 'bullish
feature that every foreign wheat country
makes a good report of the growing wheat.
All of them promise an average to a full
crop. The decidedly 'bullish' feature Is the
condition of our winter wheat. What the loss
will he cannot now be determined, but It Is
eerlous. If we produce but 300,000.000 bushels
of winter wheat and a full crop of spring
wheat, and crops abroad come in full aver
age, do the present prices reflect the situa
tion? That's the conundrum."

EASTERN MARKETS.
NEW YORK STOCK MARKET.
NEW YORK, April 25.â Dealings In stocks
to-day were rather dull. The market was gen
erally heavy In the forenoon, while In the after
noon strength appeared, especially among those
stocks which had been depressed early. The
Grangers initiated the Improvement coinci
dent with the weakness in wheat. London was
a f-oller of the Internationals, particularly of
Louisville â â â  Southern. Although the profes
sional element was bearish, speculation
throughout displayed an undertone of resist
ance which caused 'many of the trading ele
ment to reverse their tactics. Among the gen
erally inactive shares very material advances
appeared In Minneapolis and St. Louis. Detroit
Gas, Canadian Pacific and Illinois Central.
Anaconda Mining advanced sharply on con
tinued gossip connecting it with the proposed
consolidation. Sugar, Glucose and Metropoli
tan were well supported, and Tennessee Coal
more than recovered from its early weakness.
Gossip was revived of some alliance among
sugar and glucose interests, but nothing defl-'
nite wan made known In this connection, par
ties who should know being non-committal. In
the money and sterling exchange markets
steadiness prevailed at practically unchanged
quotations. Strength prevailed In the dealings
until near the close, when realizing, especially
In Manhattan and other transportation shares,
cut off part of the advance, leaving the mar
ket weak, although a number of shares retained
good advances on the day.
Business In bonds was well distributed to
day and prices were in the main higher. Total
Bales, $3.C55.000.

In l ho bid price.

LONDON MARKET.
NEW YORK, April 25.â The Commercial Ad
vertiser's London financial cablegram says:
The markets here were Irregular to-day, the j
features being Canadian Pacific and coppers.
Anacondas reached 13%. The single option to
end in May was 13-16 for longer dates; the
rates were prohibitive. Utahs were 11%, bid
10%. It Is said here that Standard Oil inter
ests have now secured a majority of the
shares of the Anaconda mine and are taking
Boston next. It is reported that Mr. Rocke
feller and the Lewshonns have an agent here
with Â£2,500,000 credit to acquire control of the
iron market, like the copper. A squeeze Is
1 certainly proceeding. Americans opened tirm.
; went a half point above parity and then
dwindled till the close. In the street they re
covered. The general contango was 4Â®4%. On
St. Paul the rate was 3%. Canadian Pacific
opened at â â .'Â» and closed at 96. it Is said
that the company has got the money to buy tho
Manitoba bonds. Sliver was hard. Ninety-five
thousand dollars In gold bars was bought by
the bank.

THE STOCK MARKET.
There was a lively business in local securi
ties on the morning session of the Bond Ex
change. Oceanic Steamship advanced to $76@75,
Hawaiian Commercial to $109 25, Hutchinson to
$35@36 12^. Giant Powder to $71 75 and Mutual
Electric to $17. Paauhau weakened off to $43
and Contra Costa Water sold from $72 50 down
to $71 75. Sales were large.
Mining stocks sold rather higher In the mid
dle of the day, but weakened off again in the
afternoon. The telegram from the pump said:
"Elevator working steadily. Water In- the 17."0
level winze Is 32 feet 4 Inches below the sta
tion."
The annual meeting of the Scorpion Minlrfj
Company baa â¢ been called for May 8.
The Salmon River Mining Company of Mon
tana has levied an assessment of .'_â¢ cents per
share, delinquent May 24.
The Grand Victory Mining Company of Fall
Creek, Nevada County, has levied an assess
ment of Me per share, delinquent May 25.
Superintendent Ryan of the Hale it Norcross
mine reports as- follows: "No work was done In
the mine during the week. During the week
ending April 21 the water in our incline has
fallen 2.7 feet vertically; total, 22 feet 5 Inches
on the incline, or 14.62 feet vertically, since the
pump at the C. & C. shaft started -on April 1.
The water is now 71 feet 7 Inches below our
incline station at the Sutro tunnel connection."

NOTICE TO MARINERS.
A branch of the United States Hydrographic
Office, located In the Merchants' Exchange, is
maintained in San Francisco for the benefit of
mariners without regard to nationality and
free of expense.
Navigators are cordially Invited to visit the
office, where complete sets of charts and sail
ing directions of the world are kept on hand
for comparison and reference, and the latest
information can always be obtained regarding
lights, dangers to navigation and all matters
of interest to ocean commerce.
The time ball on the tower of the new Ferry
building at the foot of Market street is hoisted
about ten minutes before noon and dropped at
noon, 120 th meridian, by telegraphic signal re
ceived fach day from the United States Naval
Observatory, Mare Island. Cal.
A notice stating whether the ball was dropped
on time or giving the error, if any, is published
in the morning papers the following day.
C. G. CALKINS.
Lieutenant. U. S. N.. in charge.

BERKELEY GOT THE AX,
STAFORD HAS THE FENGE

STANFORD UNIVERSITY, April 25.â As the result
of a daring expedition undertaken last night by
twenty-two Stanford students the far-famed
B< rkeley fence, the traditional seat of the seniors,
rests new at Palo Alto in the possession of the
supporters of the cardinal.
The mammoth wooden "C" was removed from its sup
ports at 2:15 a. m., loaded on a wagon and brought around
the bay, arriving at the university about 1 p. m. The party
in charge of the trophy was met near Alviso by a large
body of Stanford students and escorted to the campus.
As the university was approached scores of men joined
the procession at different points along the road. By the
time the entrance t<: the college was reached the escort
numbered about sno. some being on wheels, in carriages
and buses, while ott-.ers were on foot.
The enterprise waÃ decided on yesterday noon. Most
of those who were to participate left on the afternoon
trains for Oakland. Four students, however, conducted
the wagon which was to cany the trophy to Stanford
d the bay.
-While the main party was waiting there to proceed to
Berkeley half of the members left for Piedmont, where
they secured a wagon. On entering Berkeley the difficul
ties began, for two special policemen, hired to watch the
university premises, followed the rig. The Stanford men
were In d< spalr. Then a plan to triok the officers was
worked out. As the night was dark, two men were to be
dropped from the wagon as every corner was rounded. At
last, when but four occupants were left, the policemen
overtook the men and searched the wagon. Nothing was
found and their mimls were made easy when they were
told the affair was a Berkeley D. K. E. initiation.
After apologising for his foolish alarm The policeman
told the men that he had believed them from Stanford on
ittempt to recapture the ax. "He Beemed to bo
prejudiced against the Stanford men." paid one of the
party "for he made terrible tin-eats against us and do
's he would 'jug 1 every Stanford student." The
campus watchman was led astray by two of the students
in a buggy, which they drove over the Berkeley hills.
In the meantime the majority at the party wasftt work
on th'- fence. It was taken In its entirety and carted to
? which was in waiting near Haywards. After
the transfer the triumphal procession toward Palo Alto
was begun. It consumed over ten hours.
The students heard the news with th<=> wilaest delight.
Tn an instant, every classroom and lab< ratory was de-
I. The band was summoned and the "jollying up"

THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, WEDNESDAY, APRIL 26, 1899.
TJiE .HISTORIC FEME.
BERKELEY. April 25.â The ax feud between Stan
ford and Berkeley is not over. Failing in an at
tempt to recover their stolen mascot by raiding
one of the fraternity houses of the amiverslty
town at 3 o'clock in the morning, some days ago,
the Stanford men have avenged their loss by stealing the
senior fence from the Berkeley campus and carting it
bodily down to Palo Alto. This work of retaliation they
carried out successfully last night In a manner quite simi
lar to the previous raid.
In the dead of night a small band of cardinal enthusi
asts stole into town, bringing with them a heavy wagon.
Their point of rendezvous was near the bridge at the
upper end of the campus, close by College avenue. Think
ing the coast clear they started in the direction of the
fence, but encountered the nlghtwatchman near the
library. They succeeded in passing off as
Berkeley students and engaged the officer in conversation^
for over an hour upon the library Steps. It seemed as
though their second raid were doomed to failure unless tho
watchman oould be disposed of. In despair they pretended
to start for home, and the officer seeing them depart
walked over to the lower campus. The Stanford men
watched their chance, and hastening toward the fence
lifted it out of the ground, broke it Into sections and load
ing it on the wagon In waiting hurried away with tho
spoils. It was all done noiselessly, without any alarm
being Riven.
When the U. C. men came to recitations this morning
they were highly amused at the cardinal strategy. One
and all declared the josh to be on Stanford. The senior
fence was a weather-beaten concern erected by tho class
of '98, and consisted of a few planks arranged on supports
in the shape of a big C. It was not a college affair, but
belonged only to the class of '98, and had already out
lived Its usefulness when the class graduated a year ago.
Arrangements had been made to tear the thing down be
fore the end of the present term, as its presence could be
tolerated no longer. The Berkeley students smiled as they
thought of Stanford's success in ridding them of an old
eyesore, and they are congratulating themselves on seeing
it go. A section of them, .disposed to carry the josh still
further, are raising a subscription to send the cardinal the
sum of 50 cents, declaring that the expre.ssage ought not
to have been done gratuitously.
nil-important question occupied the whole
of the afternoon and lasted until a late
hour last night.
At Ita conclusion a hallot was taken on
the advisability of changing, the location
of the seminary from its present site to
one in Berkeley, as near as possible to
the State University grounds. The ballot
was almost unanimously in favor of the
change, the faculty and trustees not vot-
Ing, as they had called the conference as
advisory to themselves, ami final decision
ng with them In any event.
This action of the joint meeting was
offi< lally brought before the board of
trustees at its meeting this afternoon,
and after further discussion a unanimous
vote was taken committing the institu
tion to the change of location.
The property on which the Pacific The
ological Seminary has been located for
a third of a century consists of between
eight and nine acres on a knoll at the
head of Webster street, between Tele
graph avenue and Broadway, and above
St. Mary's College. It will make one of
the finest residence properties in the city,
and is to be sold by the trustees in ac
cordance with orders. . .
The board of trustees is composed of
Rev. John K. McLean. D.D. (president),
Rev. W. C. Pond, D.D. (secretary). Ed
ward P. Flint, James M. Haven (treas
urer), Rev. Henry E. Jewett, James E.
Ager. Rev. Samuel H. Willey, D.D., Na
thaniel P. Cole. Rev. Walter Frear,
Charles Holbrook, Rev.' George Mooar,
D.D. James L. Barker, Rev. Leland D.
Rathbone, George W. Scott and Rev.
George C. Adams, D.D.
Rev. Dr. McLean has been president of
the board for the past twenty years, and
managing president of the institution for
five years. He and the faculty heartily
approve the new plan, and would gladly
welcome any and all other, similar instl
tions to their neighborhood and a free
participation in all the advantages.
"Though we will leave our present
beautiful site with regret," said Presi
dent McLean to a' Call reporter after
the meeting, "we feel that new condl
It Wasn't JVlucK
of arx Old
Fence, Ar\\j-
KoW, Sa\j tKe
U. G. Boys.
tinns bring new responsibilities; and
sentiment must be sacrificed for the best
interests .of education. For several
months the question has oeen under dis
cussion whether the usefulness and real
power of the seminary might not he in
creased by placing it in proximity
to the State University. Our students
have for years been taking more and
more studies from the university and we
are anxious that we should avail our
selves more largely of the facilities there.
â  The changes taking place all the while
in society require the minister to be edu
cated more broadly than twenty-five
years ago. He needs to add to the old
standard branches some knowledge of the
new questions that have come forward
In . the life of this community. Upon the
other hand, as the university develops it
is putting into its own curriculum a great
many things which an isolated seminary
would have to furnish for Itself."
The graduating- exercises were also held
at the seminary to-day, the chief fea
ture being an able address by Rev. George
B. Hatch of Berkeley on "The Person
ality of the Preacher." Among the grad
uates were F. C. Krause of Washington;
also C. K. Takeda, who. after he shall
have concluded a two years' post gradu
ate course in divinity studies at Yale, will
return to Japan. The class was the
smallest in several years, owing to some
new adjustment in the curriculum. The
curriculum of the institution is now to be
materially changed and a committee has
been appointed to consider this particu
lar subject. The new features will be
partially put into operation with the
opening of the next seminary year which
will hereafter be at the same time as that
of the State University.
The alumni held a meeting this after
noon and thoroughly approved, by resolu
tion, the policy the seminary has adopted
and renewing assurances of Its hearty co
operation. '--.--

NO LIQUOR AT
RAILROAD DEPOTS
Sweeping Order Issued to Go
Into Effect on June 1.
OAKLAND OFFICE SANT FRANCISCO CALL, 908 Broadway. April 25.
ONE of the .Host sweeping orders ever issued by the Southern Pacific
Company concerning the behavior and well-being of its employes goes
into effect June 1. It forbids tfie sale of liquor at restaurants connected
with the depots where such restaurants are rented from the railroad
company. As nearly every restaurant on the Western division is
rented from the railroad company this order has a very wide scope.
For many years the Southern Pacific has habitually .^siied orders for
bidding its employ-s from entering saloons when on duty and sometimes when
off duty. In many instances the trainmen have obtained their liquor at the
bars attached to the restaurants along their runs, and eight years ago the
first command was sent out that such places should not bo visited by em
ployes. Since then the number of saloon-restaurants at depots has grown
and the trainmen if they had felt inclined have found every facility to dis
obey the orders of the company.
The order just issued will cut off a large portion of the revenue of the
railroad company, for at the several stations rents for restaurants have
largely increased after the proprietors have obtained licenses to sell liquor.
Originally the rent paid at Nlles, for instance, was $10. Gradually it has crept
up till now the proprietor pays $40, and he has declared that when the order
goes into effect he will have to go out of business, for a large part of his in
come is derived from selling liquor. Of course, not only to trainmen, but to
passengers.
At several of the depots on this division men who have started In these
saloon-restaurants have grown rich on their profits, for it is customary to
charge two or three prices for drinks, and there was also a large business
done in flasks. Occasionally when a one-day or Sunday excursion passes
through some of these depots where saloon-restaurants are maintained hun
dreds of dollars have been spent while the train remained a few minutes!
with the result that before the passengers reached Oakland or San Francisco
there would be several wrecked cars and broken limbs.
Owen Rooney, the proprietor of the restaurant at the pi^-r. Bays his tface
does not come within the scope of the order, as his restaurant is away from
the depot and not under the jurisdiction entirely of the railroad company.

NEILL WON FROM
TRACEY ON A FOUL
OTTS KNOCKED OUT JOHNSON IN
THE FIBST ROUND.
â â â â
Martin Denny Proved to Be an Easy-
Mark for Hard-Hitting
Jimmie Lawlor.
Professor Walter Watson made a ten
strike last evening as a judge of pugilistic
contests by awarding 1 the fight between
Al Neill and Tom Tracey to the former
on a foul '
The pugilists fought twenty rounds, and
in the last round Tracey, having been
cautioned several times during the mill
not to hit in a clinch, after being ordered
to break, again transgressed the rule and
the referee quickly ordered the contest
ants to their respective corners and, plac
ing his hand on Neill's shoulders, said,
"You are the winner." The decision was
received by the gallery and pit with
rounds of cheers for Neill and Referee
Watson.
The contest was a good one. It pleased
all present excepting those who take no
interest in the gaone of fisticuffs unless
it is composed of wild slogging, with facts
painted with gore. Neill proved that he
is a most promising candidate for honors
against the best men who represent the
145-pound class of professional fighters.
Although in a number of rounds Tracey
had the better of the contest on points, it
must be said that the battle was pretty
even up to the nineteenth round, al
though there were several hundred people
among the crowd who could see only one
man in the fight and his name was Neill.
Several times Tracey stopped Neill's
leads with his gloves, but the friends of
the local man thought that Neill landed
each time.
Tracey was In the pink of condition,
and those who witnessed the mill saw
him put up the best argument since his
arrival from Australia, but he could not
draw his opponent out, notwithstanding
the many fancy maneuvers he executed
during the contest. Tracey tried every
trick he knew of to get Neill to lower his
guard and give him (Tracey) an oppor
tunity to land the right on "the jaw, but
Neill wu.-; cool and kept the vulnerable
point w.-H protected, excepting in one
or two hot rallies, when Tracey sent home
a right upper cut that dazed the receive r.
In the first round Tracey somewhat be
wildered Neill by his rapid movements
and beautiful side stepping. He planted
his left glove on Neill's face several
times, but the blows were light. In the
second round Neill grew more confident
and was willing to tafce the love laps
in exchange for one or two right hand
smashes at any part of Tracey's body.
In a mix-up Neill landed a left swing on
Tracey's neck and in breakaway he Hung
the Australian down. In the third Neill
got home three stiff punches on Tracey's
neck and body, but caught a hard left
smash on the nose. I-n breaking from a
right clinch both fighters sprawled on the
floor.
1 Miring the fourth round Tracey jumped
around Neill like a grasshopper, but was'
stopped] suddenly by a stiff body blow,
which was followed by rapid exchanges
at close range, out of which Tracey came
a surprised man, as Neill sent him down
with a left-hander on the neck. The Aus
tralian was up quickly and smiled
blandly.
In the fifth, sixth and seventh rounds
Tracey changed his style of fighting and
resorted to tactics of stabbing his oppo
nent in the stomach at long range. By
this method he expected that Neill would
lower his guard for the chin, but the
game did not pan out as Tracey expected.
Still, Tracoy waa rapidly scoring up
points.
In the eighth round the Australian
came very near bagging the home man.
After a series of exchanges Neill made
a wicked left swing for Tracey's pointed
jaw, which the Australian ducked like a
flash and then caught Neill a hard short
arm righthand punch on the jaw. which
caused Neill's knees to bend under him.
Tracey shook Neill from a clinch and
tried hard to end the game, but failed in
the attempt. Neill went to his corner
very Kroppy. The time clock saved him.
In the tenth Tracey was hissed for hit
! ting in the clinches.
The eleventh round was the most ex
' citing of the evening. The fighters mixed
I matters up and Neill happened to catch
i Tracey a hard right swing on the ear
which staggered the Australian. Neill,
seeing his opportunity, went at Tracey
wildly, rind the Australian, by remark
ably clever ducking and clinching, got
back his senses and caught Neill a fear
ful smash on the jaw which sent Neill
staggering- and clinching. Tracey again
tried to finish Neill, and during "the ex
citement the sports above and below
were on their feet and shouting like mad
men. Traecy hit Neill another hard
smash in the mouth as the gong sounded
for corners.
After this the fighters were pretty well
pumped, and the exchanges at closerange
were not so numerous until the final
round, when Tracey again tried to land
his right and failing in the attempt he
! struck Neill. in a clinch and lost the fight.
Billy Otts knocked out Charley Johnson
in the first round, and Jimmy Lawlor pot
a decision from Joe Acton over the Aus
tralian. Martin Denny, who did not seem
tn be trying to bag his man very hard,
although Law'or fought well.

ACTIVE DEMAND
ear IMPROVED
REAL PROPERTY
Many Buyers Seeking
Investments.
A FEW BIG DEALS CLOSED
AGENTS ARE WELL SATISFIED
WITH THE OUTLOOK.
Temporary Falling Off in the Number
of Sales Recorded Not Due to
Any Reaction in the
Market.
The real estate market has been very
quiet during the past week, but while |
actual transactions were light the de
mand for Improved, income-producing 1
property continues active, with a num- .
ber of good-sized deals, on the verge of
being consummated. The sale of the
Tubbs mansion on Jackson street, near
Steiner, which was concluded early last'j
woek, wae the largest single transaction :
noted. Shainwald, Buckbee & Co. also
closed a deal by which Rudolph Bpreck
els becomes the owner of a triangular
piece of property with a frontage of 74.6
feet on O'Farrell' street, with a depth of
337.6 feet and having a 60-foot frontage
on Elwood alley. The big piece of
ground was owned by a number of par- j
ties and part of it was secured some time
ago. The transfer of the O'Farrell street
frontage, however, was not made until
Monday. The tire purchase represents I
an investment of >â " 000. It is the pur- !
pose of the new owner to erect a first- j
class building on the property, although
â¢it may be some time before active opera
tions are begun.
Nearly twenty building .permits have
been taken out during- the past week, the
amounts involved aggregating between
J:Â«t,!VVi and $100,000. This a in addition. to ,
several big buildings that are soon to go
up, including the new opera-house on
Geary street and the big store and office
building on the corner of Geary and
Stockton streets. The latter is to be |
erected by C. C. Black, the capitalist,
who owns the property, and the work or i
tearing down the old buildings that now
encumber the site is pressing rapidly.
The Kit is 137x137, and Mr. Black will put
up a building that will be R credit to the
city and the location. The foundation
will be put in for a ten-story building, al
though it is probable that the structure
at present contemplated will not be over
six stories in height. It will cost about
$100,000.
The new building to be erected by
Henry Kahn on Ellis street, between
Powell and Mason, will cost in the neigh-.
borhood of $175,000.
A. J. Rich & Co. yesterday executed a
leasehold of the store and basement at j
the corner of Grant avenue and Geary |
street to R. P. Davis of Sacramento. |
The lease is for live years at a monthly
rental of ?550. The lessoxs are Davis
Brothers; no relation to the lessee.
Loans have been fairly good during the
past week, but only one for an unusual- j
Fy large amount was recorded, that of i
the German Savings Bank, of $67,000 to
John and Hannah McDonald on the prop
erty at the northwest corner of Devlsa
ri< ro and Eddy. -

NOTICE.
To All Whom It May Concernâ Notice Is
hereby given that on the 17th day of April,
Ifc99. the Bakersfield and Los Angeles Kail-
way Company filed its application In writing,
in the office of the Clerk of the Superior
Count of the City and County of San Fran-
cisco, addressed to the Superior Court of said
county, making application for the voluntary
dissolution of the Bakersfleld . and Los An-
geles Railway Company, which said petition
and application stated that at a meeting of
the stockholders of said company on the 13th
day of April. 1 V 99, culled for the purpose of
passing upon the question. It was resolved to
have a voluntary dissolution of the corpora-
tion, and that such resolution was passed by
a two-thirds vote of all the stockholders, and
that all the claims and demands against the
corporation, of every kind, nature and descrip-
tion, have been fully paid and discharged.
Wherefore, It was ordered by the said Su-
perior Court upon the filing of said petition
and application that the undersigned, as
Clerk of said Court, give a notice of such ap-
plication by publication in some newspaper
published in the City and County of San
Francisco, for a period of thirty-five days.
Therefore, all persons having any interest In
the matter of said application or desiring to
oppose the granting of the same are here- j
by notified to file In my office, on or before
the 22d day of May. 1599. any and all objec-
tions which they may have to the granting of j
raid petition.
In testimony whereof, I have her.-unto set my
hand and the official seal of said court this
the 17th day of April, 1899.
(Seal) WILLIAM A. DEANE,
County Clerk and ex-Offlcio Clerk of the
Superior Court. .
By E. M. THOMPSON,
Deputy County Clerk and ex-Offlclo Deputy
Clerk of the Superior Court.

lintfuent Tax List for 1898.
OFFICE OF THE CLERK OF THE
Board of Supervisors of the City and
County of San Francisco, April 25, ivu.
In accordance with Resolution No. 2626
(Fourth Series* of the Board of Supervis-
ors, SEALED PROPOSALS will be re-
ceived In O] he Board of Su-
pervisors on MONDAY AFTERNOON,
'May 8, IV'J'.<.1 V 'J'.<. from 2:30 to 3 o'clock, for the
printing, publishing and distributing of
.the delinquent tax list for the year 1898,
to contain the names of the persons and
; a description of the property delinquent
and t'ne amount of taxes and costs due
opposite each name, and a notice unless
:ii. taxes delinquent, together with the
costs and percentage, are paid, the prop-
erty upon winch said taxes are a lien
will be sold at public auction. Such
publication to be made and thoroughly j
distributed on a business day, at least
onci in each week, for three successive
wei ks, in a pamphlet form as a supple-
ment to a -daily newspaper of general cir-
culation, published In the city .and
county of San Francisco, In the
English language, and said supple-
ment must be sent and distrib-
uted on the days specified with every
number of the paper issue. i and distrib-
uted, by carriers, by mail or otherwise.
The first issued td be so distributed on or
before the first day of June, 1899', and the
others weekly thereafter, and to desigr
the time and place of sale and in all '
onform to the provisions of the
Pol ode and to be done under the
direction and to the satisfaction of Ed-
ward !. Sheehan, Tax Collector of said
City and County; also, to leave at the Tax
Collector's office not less than one thou-
sand (loOO; copies of each issue before any
demand on the Treasury therefor will
be approved. Within one week after the
tirst publication of said list fifty (50)
copies thereof, printed upon one side only
of a quality of paper which can be written
upon with ink, to be satisfactory to said
Tax Collector, to be delivered at his office
for use in the office. Also fifty (50) cloth-
bound copies, interleaved, to be delivered
to the Tax Collector's office within two
weeks after the first publication.
The Tax Collector must have the privi-
lege at any and all times during the exe-
cution of the said contract of witnessing-
the same and giving instructions relative
thereto either personally or through his
chief deputy, or any deputy he may desig-
nate.
All proofs must be read and submitted
for correction as follows:
Firstâ Proofs must be tirst read by copy
and corrected by the printer.
Secondâ Submitted in slips to the Tax
Collector for correction.

alterations are made by said Tax Collec-
tor or his chief deputy, or deputy, a cor-
rected revise must be presented to and
approved by him prior to the form being
struck off, and as the work progresses
when a form is struck off a copy of the
same to be filed with said Tax Collector.
Payment to be made as hereinafter pro-
vided and the bill presented to be veri-
fied by the affidavit of a proper and re-
sponsible person acceptable to the Com-
mittee on Printing and Salaries of the
Board of Supervisors, said affidavit to be
made before a notary public in and for
the said City and County, and to set
forth that the contract for printing, pub-
lishing and distributing the Delinquent
Tax List for the year 189S, has .been fully
and faithfully executed and accomplished
in strict conformity to and with these
specifications.
The contractor or contractors will re-
ceive a demand upon the Treasury of the
said City and County for the payment of
the contract price in United States gold
coin, when satisfactory evidence is fur-
â  nished the said Committee on Printing
and Salaries that the said contract has
been fully and faithfully executed and ac-

PROPOSALS
For Burial of Indigent Dead.
, OFFICE OF THE CLERK OF THE
Board of Supervisors of the City and
County of San Francisco, April 25, 1599.
In accordance with Resolution No. 262S
(Fourth Series) of the Board of Super-
: visors, SEALED PROPOSALS will be re-
: ceived in open session of the" Board of
! Supervisors on MONDAY AFTERNOON,
May 8, 189 ft, from 2:30 to 3 o'clock, for
the interment of such of the indigent
dead as are. required to be buried at the
charge of the City and County of San
Francisco, Including the City and County
Smallpox Hospital, for the term of one
â  year, beginning July 1, 1599, and ending
June 30, 1900, in such cemetery or ceme-
teries in San Mateo County wherein
burials may be allowed.
NOTICE.â The coffins to be made of
three-quarter-inch surface redwood clear
lumber, to be lined with muslin and pil-
lowed inside, and to be shellacked and
varnished outside, and the tops to be
fastened down with not less than six
screws. In all cases where persons have
died from smallpox or other contagious
diseases the coffin shall be lined with No.
10 zinc instead of with muslin, and her-
metically sealed, and all coffins used
, must be subject to the inspection and ap-
proval of the officer in charge of the in-
stitution from which the body of the
\ decedent is to be buried, and' must in all
cases be taken to such institution for the
purpose of having the deceased placed
therein.
No bid will be considered from any par-
ty who has not been engaged in the un-
dertaking. business in this City and Coun-
, ty and conducted paid business in a rep-
! utable manner for a period of at least six
; months immediately preceding the date
hereof.
The grave to be excavated to a depth
of at least six feet.
No body to be removed from the insti-
! tution where it lies awaiting burial with-
I in a period of twenty-four hours after
' death, except by express order of the
Superintendent or officer in charge of
said institution.
The contractor will be required in-all
cases on the death of the indigent person
from Infectious disease to Inter the body
of said decedent within such time as may
be designated by notice from the Superin-
tendent or officer in charge of the institu-
tion where the death may have occurred,
and in all other cases within thirty-six
hours after notification by the proper of-
ficer.
The contractor will further be required
to convey the bodies of decedents com-
mitted to his charge for interment direct
, from the institution from which he re-
ceives the same to the cemetery, and then
and there bury the same. And will not
be permitted upon any consideration to
convey such bodies to any intermediate
resting-place, or to keep the same in his
custody for a longer period than that re-
quired- for their transit to the cemetery.
Should the friends of any decedent ap-
ply at "the institution where such deced-
ent may be lying awaiting burial, for per-
mission to bury the same, and prove to
the Superintendent or officer in charge of
said institution that their purpose is to
provide a decent burial, and that they
have no intention and will not allow the
1 body to be used for the purpose of dissec-
tion, then and in that case the body of
said deceased shall be delivered to said
friends, who shall take charge thereof
, ''and make the necessary arrangements for
the funeral with any undertaker they
i may thin-k fit: but in such event the con-
tractor aforesaid shall have no claim
against the city for the burial of said de-
cedent.
The party to whom the contract may
be awarded shall be required, in all cases,
to procure from the Superintendent of the
cemetery wherein the burials have been
made a certificate of the proper burial of
each person under the provisions of his
1 contract and of these specifications; and
such certificate must, in all cases, be at-
tached to the demand of said contractor
when presented to the Board of Super-
i visors for approval, and must state the
names of the decedents and the institu-
tions from which the body was procured.
In case of the violation of any of the
above conditions, the contract shall be
terminated by said City and County.
The party to whom this contract is
awarded will be required, prior to or at
the time of the execution of said contract,
to pay the cost of advertising this notice
in three daily newspapers, and to also
I pay the cost of the publication in the of-
ficial- newspaper of the Resolution award-
ing him the contract.
All interments of indigent persons the
I expenses of which must be borne by the
City and County (other than those from
1 any of the public institutions) must be
â  authorized by the Health Officer issuing
! a permit therefor, and all bills for such
interments to be approved by the Health
: Officer prior to being presented to this
Board. All demands for the burial of In-
-1 digent persons from any of the public in-
stitutions to be approved by the Superin-
tendent of the institution at which the
death occurs, prior to being presented to
this Board.
Bidders will estimate and state a speci-
fic amount for each interment.
In order to preserve the uniformity and
to facilitate the award, the Board has
resolved to receive no bids unless made
upon blank forms prepared by the com-
I mittee, and a certified check on some
i bank in the City and County of San Fran-
cisco for the sum cf five hundred ($500)
dollars, in favor of and made payable to
the Clerk of the Board of Supervisors,
must be deposited by the bidder, with his
bid, conditioned that if the proposal is
accepted and the contract awarded, and
if the bidder shall fail or neglect to pay
the printing charges, execute the con-
tract and give a bond- of five thousand
($5000) dollars, required within six days af.
ter the award is made, then, and in that
case the said sum shall bo collected and
paid into the City and County Treasury
by said Clerk as liquidated damages for
such failure and neglect.
Blanks furnished by the Clerk..
The Board reserves the right to reject
all bids if the public good so require.
JNO. A. RUSSELL. Clerk.

"PUT UP YOUR
HAMMERS," SAYS
JULIUS KAHN
More State Pride Is
Needed Here.
ALL SHOULD WORK TOGETHER
TH.b CONGRESSMAN STATES
SOME PLAIN TRUTHS.
Lectures Entertainingly on "Califor
nia â Past, Present and Future,"
for the Benefit of Young
Men's Institute.
Congressman Julius Kahn. lectured last
right before a large audience in Odd Fel
lows' Hall under the auspices of Stross
mayer and rke Councils, Young Men's
Institute, The lecture chosen for his
theme was "California, Past, Present and
Future." lie has an abounding faith in
this State, believing it will shortly rank
as one of the most prosperous sections
of the world. Speaking of California as
it is to-day he said:
If the people of California had more
Stato pride it would be of inestima
ble value to the State. We all love it,
but we need to forget our petty jeal
ousies and our envy of Individuals, by
which we stand in the way of our
great enterprises. We have too many
men with "hammers out" ready to hit
a head wherever they see. one. There
is too much personal spite and per
sonal jealousy which warps men's
judgment. Whenever any one pro
poses a great movement the tirst
question is "what is ho going to make
out o" it and what does he want?"
fOur citizens should cultivate State
pride and help the State along. They
should bury the dog-in-the-manger,
childish, peevish policy ' which has
characterized them in the pastâ cast
aside their policy of "if I cannot, thou
shalt not."
The lack of State pride is respon
sible for much of the backwardness of
this country. There should be no
sectionalism hen?â no Northern Cali- j
fornia, no Southern Californiaâ as '
though this State was divided into two
commercial nations struggling for su
premacy, instead of one great State
wit! one end nnd one object in view
that of prosperity and progress. That
man should be expelled and ostra
cized from the. State who would stir
up any sectional differences. The fu
ture is brighter than ever was dreamed
of. As has been said by able men. the
future commerce of the world will be :
found on the Pacific Ocean. This is
â¢ about to be realized, and California
will come in for the greater share of
The speaker traced the history of Cal- i
ifornia back several centuries, conveying
a mass of historical facts in â  a highly
entertaining maner.
â  â¦ â 
A flow has been discovered which is
red in the sunlight and white in the
moonlight.

THREE FIRMS TO
BE LICENSED TO
RUN POOLROOMS
Trustee Sylva Alters
His Opinion.
KINGSLEY & CO. WELCOMED
SAUSALITO WILL SOON BE A
"WIPE OPEN" TOWN.
Talk of Special Elections and Disin
corporation, by Which Super
visors Were Delayed,
Is Dropped.
The talk of disincorporate at Sausa
lito in order to delude the Board of Su
pervisors of Marln County into thinking
there will be no need for them to pass an
ordinance virtuaJly prohibitive of the pool
room traffic in that "wide open" town
has be^n entirely dropped. Trustee Sylva,
the "boss," to whom all searchers after
wealth by chance in Marln must come
with their petition, has so decreed. More
over, there is to be no special election to
be held to determine whether or not the
people of Sausalito want the poolroom*
this year.
"The people voted seven to one for th<>
poolrooms last year," said Sylva, "and
there Is no legitimate reason to suppose
they have changed their minds so quickly
as many think they have. We have
finally determined to hold no election, but
go ahead and license the rooms this year
the same as last."
Another radical departure has also been
determined upon. Last year the whole
Board of Trustees, composed of Sylva,
Thomas and Ambjornsenâ Dickinson and
Crumpton proving mere ornaments so far
as the passage of an ordinance is con
cernedâdecided that three poolrooms
would be too many and it went at that.
The wires of Broyer, Glynn & Hum
phrey's establishment were cut and there
was a merry row all around, but the lit
tle boss of Marin had hiÃ way, as he al
ways does In Sausalito. Now a change
has for some reason or other come o'er
the spirit of the trio's dream and three
ri.omf are to be licensed. Nobody knows
the reason why this change has been de
cided upon, but a peculiar fact is that
nine out of every ten will make the same
conjecture.
Harvey & Co., Humphrey & Co. and
Kingsley & Co. are the titles of the firms
that will hereafter sell pools on Eastern
races in Rausalito. So far as the will of
the populace is concerned, this move of
Sylva will he welcomed. Their i'lr-a is,
the more the merrier, but there are a few
who are opposed to the chanK*\ among
them being Billy" McMillan and "John
ny" Peters, two saloon-keepers, who have
a minority report io make. But their op
position cuts no figureâ there will be three
poolrooms just thp same. Sylva has so
decreed, and his two satellites can be de
pendpd on to stand firm.
JOHNSON'S FIRST ORDERS.
Acting < mander of the Grand
Army Takes Hold.
CINCINNATI, April 25.â The first two
orders of the acting commander in chi^f
of the Grand Army of the Republic, W.
C. Johnson, the senior vice commandpr
In chief, have been issued. The first one
recites the action of the executive coun
cil in providing that the. place of the dead
commander in chief Sexton should not be
filled until the end of his term, hut that
the duties of the office should be per
formed by the senior vice commander in
chief. It announces that his office shall
be at 321 Pike building, Cincinnati. The
other order is the annual one concern
ing the Memorial day. May 30. The order
calls attention to the provision of the
thirtieth national encompment that Presi
dent Lincoln's Gettysburg address be
made a feature of Memorial day exer
cises, and directs commanders of posts to
have it read In connection with the exer
ciaee of the day.
